# Advanced Accessible PDFs

> Accessible PDF files are files that can be read by a screen reader by users who are visually impaired. While you can’t just push a button to make a PDF accessible, with the tools available in Microsoft Word and Adobe InDesign, you can make sure that any PDF you create is a valid accessible PDF file. Prioritizing accessibility doesn't mean you can't leverage the powerful tools the PDF format offers

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253) | 6h 46m | Advanced | 36K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

#### [Is your document accessible?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/is-your-document-accessible?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/is-your-document-accessible?u=76281980&t=0)** - A few years ago I was speaking at a conference about the benefits of the PDF file format and during that conference, a woman in the back of the room raised her hand and said, "That's great, but is your document accessible?" I had no idea what she meant. Now, when we talk about document accessibility everybody wants to hear that all you have to do is push a button to make an accessible PDF file, and unfortunately that's not the case. Actually, it's far from it. I'm Chad Chelius, and in this course I'll show you how to work with complex tables, automated fixes, fillable PDF [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md), PDF UA compliance, and a lot more. So sit back, put your headphones on, and get ready to follow along with me as I walk you through how to solve those lingering remediation issues that you've been dealing with.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** pdf (4)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - a (1)

#### [What you should know](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before we dive into this course, there's some things I'd like you to know about my approach when creating this course. In my first course, [Creating Accessible PDFs](Creating%20Accessible%20PDFs.md), I took a workflow based approach in teaching the course. Each chapter walked you through the process of remediating a PDF file pretty much from start to finish. So the course was very logical in its structure and it had a natural flow to it. I did this because when you're learning how to make accessible PDF files, it can be a bit overwhelming and I wanted your learning experience to be a logical approach to make the learning process easier. If you haven't watched my first course, [Creating Accessible PDFs](Creating%20Accessible%20PDFs.md) yet, I highly encourage you to do so. Unlike my first course, this course takes a different approach to learning PDF accessibility. PDF accessibility can be a complex beast and discovering nuances and other issues in your files is quite common. This course attempts to address those issues that you may have encountered and shows you how to fix them to make your files compliant and to create a good [user experience](../../Skills/Web%20Development/User%20Experience%20(UX).md) for people who use assistive technology. Because I'm addressing nuance issues in this course, it doesn't follow as much of a flow as the first course did, but rather contains small nuggets of useful information that can help you address common issues in your files. I'm really happy that you're here and really excited that you want to take your PDF remediation skills to the next level.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Shortcuts for walking the tags tree](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/shortcuts-for-walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/shortcuts-for-walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=0)** - The task of walking the tags tree is an important step in the remediation process as it accomplishes a couple of important tasks. One, it ensures that elements in your document are tagged properly, and two it ensures that the tag order is appropriate. In this video I'd like to show you some helpful keyboard shortcuts that you can use when walking the tags tree. I'm starting this video with the 01_01 document open. And to access the tags tree, I'm going to come over here to the left side of my screen and I'm going to click on this little triangle that you'll find about halfway down on the screen. Now, if you're starting from a fresh install of Acrobat you're probably not going to see the tags pane right now. So to show it what you could do is simply right click somewhere in the navigation pane and choose tags and that is going to display the tags pane. And within there you're going to notice that all of the tags in your document are found there. Now, walking the tags tree is a pretty simple step and one of the things that I like to do is I'll start by clicking on the topmost tag, right? In my case, it's the document tag. And then to walk the tags tree, what you can do is press the down arrow key on your keyboard. And what this does is navigates vertically from one tag to the other. You can also press the up arrow key to move up in the tags tree, right?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/shortcuts-for-walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=93)** So as you're evaluating the tag structure of your document it's very common to kind of go up and down. Maybe you check something and then decide you want to go back to it and and just kind of double check it. Again, up and down Arrow key allows you to navigate up and down the tags tree. Now sometimes you'll come upon a tag, for example, right now I have the TOC tag selected. Well, the TOC tag is actually comprised of multiple tags, right? So what I can do if I want to see what's located within that tag, I can press the right arrow key on my keyboard. And what that does is it expands the tag showing all of the tags within it. So I can continue pressing the down arrow key effectively selecting the child tags of the TOC tag in this example, right? And I can press the right arrow key again to open up a TOCI tag, in which case I'm going to find in this case a link tag. I can press the right arrow key again to open that tag. Now conversely, and although this is not required by the way, but conversely, I can press the left arrow key to collapse a tag. And when you're in a nested tag, you can simply continue pressing the left arrow key to kind of back out of where you are within that nested tag. So let me just do that again.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/shortcuts-for-walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=189)** Right arrow, right arrow, right arrow. If I just left arrow, left arrow, left arrow, left arrow, I can just kind of back out of that tag structure. So, when you're evaluating your tag structure what I'm doing is as I'm down arrowing, I'm visually looking at what's getting highlighted, and again, making sure that content is being read in the order that I want it to. And then I'm also, as I get to a heading, I'll look over at the tags pane and make sure that the tag associated with that content is in fact a heading tag and more specifically, the heading tag that I want assigned to it. When I move up a little bit and I get to a smaller heading it makes sense that this is a heading three. When I move down, our audience is a bigger heading, it makes sense that that's a heading too. So again, this is the process of walking the tags tree and using those keyboard shortcuts is really, really helpful because when you get to a tag that you want to dive a little bit deeper into, you can do so very easily. Now I'll show you a few more things here. I'm just going to kind of scrub through here a little bit. I want to find a tag here. Here's a table, right? Here's a good example. So you know I already showed you the the shortcuts on your keyboard. The other thing I want to show you is that if you click on a tag, if you hold down the control key on [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) or the alt key on Mac
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/shortcuts-for-walking-the-tags-tree?u=76281980&t=283)** and you click on this disclosure triangle, it will blow the tags apart. And what I mean by that is it will open up all child tags of the selected tag and it will expand all of them so that you can see all of the children elements of that tag. Now the thing is like if you just close that table tag and you move on or whatever, and then you go back to it you'll notice that it's still expanded. So similar to what we did to expand it, if you hold down the control key on Windows or the option key on Mac and click on the disclosure triangle it will fully collapse all of those tags. And now if I just click to open it you'll see that they're no longer exploded, all of the tags are not expanded. So I hope you found these shortcuts helpful and I encourage you to leverage them when walking the tags tree in your own documents.

#### [Paragraphs that split across a page](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/paragraphs-that-split-across-a-page?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/paragraphs-that-split-across-a-page?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Depending on the source application you use to create a PDF file, the way it tags content may not be accurate or what you might expect. One example of this is when a paragraph breaks across a page, where the beginning of a paragraph is at the bottom of one page and continues at the top of another. This is very normal for us as sighted readers, but the experience for somebody using assisted technology could be very different. Fundamentally, that text should just be tagged as a single paragraph. However, sometimes our software fails and doesn't tag content appropriately. Let's take a look at an example. But, I like to remind people that to a user of assistive technology the concept of a page is fairly irrelevant, right? #### [Creating hyperlinks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-hyperlinks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Whenever possible, I highly encourage people to create hyperlinks for content in the source application because that information will get passed on to the PDF when it is created and it's really easy to do. That being said, sometimes people forget or just aren't aware that they can do it in a source application. In this video, I'd like to show you how to create a hyperlink in an existing PDF file to ensure that users of assistive technology will be able to follow that link appropriately. I should mention also that Adobe Acrobat automatically detects implicit hyperlinks in a document. By implicit links, I mean links that are spelled out using www or name@[example.com](https://example.com). Unfortunately, those automatically detected links are not accessible, so you'd need to use the method I'm about to show you to make them correct. All right, so I'm going to go ahead and jump to page 31 in my document here because on that page we have text that is meant to be a hyperlink, but it currently is not. You'll notice when I hover over it, there's no pointer finger, and if we want to dive a little bit deeper, what we could do is open our tags pane, select some text of that paragraph, and choose find tag from selection. And if you look in here, you'll notice that there's definitely no link tag to be found there. So how do we ensure that a user is going to be able to follow this particular link?
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=99)** So what we're going to do is I'm going to come up here and grab my selection tool, and using that tool, I'm going to click and drag to select the text that I want to become a link. With that text selected, I'm going to right click and choose create link. Now when you do this, you have a number of different options. For the link type, I usually change this to be an invisible rectangle. A visible rectangle is kind of ugly and not really super helpful, so the WCAG requirement for hyperlinks is that the hyperlink looks unique and they have satisfied that requirement by making it a different color and making it underlined. So the visible rectangle is not required so I'm going to change that to invisible. And then down here in the link action, I can choose what type of hyperlink I want to go to. You can see I could go to a page view, I can open an external file, or I can go to a webpage. Now, probably more often than not, you're going to be choosing open a webpage, but in this example, they're referencing a section of this document so I'm going to go ahead and go to a page view. I'm going to click the next button, and now it says, "Okay, navigate to where you want this to go and then click the set link button." So I'm just going to kind of backtrack here. I want to go to page 23 and that's where the editorial calendar is described. So I'm going to click on the set link button.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=192)** That now appropriately defines this as a link and here's the important part, guys. If you come over here to your tags pane, you'll now see that you have a link tag and if you open up that link tag, that has a link OBJR object, which stands for Object Reference, and those two elements, the link tag and the link OBJR object, is what makes this an accessible link for people using assistive technology. So by following those steps, this is going to ensure that the link is accessible and you'll also notice, of course, that even as a sighted user now when I hover over that, I get the pointer finger and if I click on it, it's going to take me to that section of the document so it's multi-beneficial to kind of follow these steps. So remember, guys, the way to know if a hyperlink is accessible is if the link is contained in a link tag and has a link OBJR object within that link tag. If it doesn't, use the technique I showed you to make sure the links are accessible.

#### [Addressing multiline hyperlinks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/addressing-multiline-hyperlinks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/addressing-multiline-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the common issues I see in PDF documents that I remediate is multi-line hyperlinks. And this is especially common when the document was created from programs like Adobe InDesign. This is a hyperlinks that begin in one line and then continues on another. And although fundamentally these should not be a problem, from an accessibility standpoint, they can be. Let's take a closer look at the problem and see how to address it. So I'm starting here with the 0106 document open and if I go to my tags pane and I find the paragraph that contains the hyperlink, you're going to notice when I open up the link tag, there are two link OBJR objects in there, okay? And again, this is one hyperlink, but the way it's created, it's broken into two. All right? Now the other place we can see that is if you open up your content pane and if you're not seeing your content pane, just right click on the navigation pane and choose content and that'll display that for you. And if you open up the page and then the annotations category, here too, you're going to see that there are two separate links. All right? So the question becomes, how do we fix this? How do we address it? Well, my preferred method is to come over here and click on the edit PDF button. And if you come up here to the link menu and choose add,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/addressing-multiline-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=95)** edit, web or document link, it'll be very visible that we have two separate links that make up this one hyperlink. So what I like to do is I'll click on one of them and simply delete it. And then what I'll do is I'll select the other one and resize it to include both parts of the hyperlink. Now I know what you're probably thinking, you're like, well, Chad, that's also making the preceding and ending text also part of the hyperlink. And you're right, from an accessibility standpoint, it's probably less of an issue than it is for a sighted user. Because for a sighted user, if I get out of editing mode here, even when I hover over the beginning of this text, it's still a hyperlink, okay? But if we take a look at our tag structure, you'll now see that there's only one link OBJR object. Actually, let me save this so that it updates. There we go. So now you can see that this link OBJR object, there's only one of them. The text is contained. The text of the hyperlink is contained within that link and it's properly structured accordingly, okay? So although it's probably maybe not quite the ideal situation for a sighted user,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/addressing-multiline-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=190)** from an accessibility standpoint, it's more accurate. The PDF format can only have a rectangular hyperlink at this point in time. If it could have a polygonal hyperlink, it would be not much of an issue, 'cause we could kind of draw the shape that we needed. But because of that limitation, we need it to be a rectangle and then of course it will encompass some of the text that is technically not part of the hyperlink. But by doing this, now this will be accessible and it'll function in a much better way than when it's split into two pieces. As you can see, fixing multi-line hyperlinks isn't particularly difficult, but it does require some time. And if you have a document that has a lot of these, you're talking a lot of time. So to avoid this problem, my best suggestion to you is to avoid multi-line hyperlinks. Take advantage of a tool such as Bitly, to shorten your hyperlinks, which can help to avoid multi-line hyperlinks altogether.

#### [Advanced techniques using the Content pane](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/advanced-techniques-using-the-content-pane?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/advanced-techniques-using-the-content-pane?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When working with elements in a PDF file, the Content Pane can provide an easy way to select items and convert them to the tag that you need. In this video, we'll leverage the Content Pane to easily select text elements and tag them appropriately. Now I have the 01_07 document open on my computer, and what I'm going to do is open the Tags Pane. And what I want to show you is that if you click on the tags, if you find the tags for these bulleted items, you'll notice that they're all tagged as P tags. Now the reason that I typically find that this happens is a lot of times, it's from Adobe InDesign. And either the person manually typed a bullet and then the text after it instead of using the bulleted list feature in InDesign, or they did properly use the bulleted list feature, and in the export tagging, mapped it to a P tag. And although fundamentally this is not going to be a barrier for people to read this content, it is a violation of WCAG 1.3.1, info and relationships. Because info and relationships says that we need to tag content as what it is. If it's a heading, tag it as a heading. If it's a list, tag it as a list. If it's a figure, tag it as a figure. And so by these bulleted items being tagged as P tags, the user of assistive technology is not going to know
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/advanced-techniques-using-the-content-pane?u=76281980&t=97)** that they're reading a list of items. > [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [Security and accessibility](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/security-and-accessibility?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/security-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] When applying security in a PDF file, special care must be taken to ensure users of assistive technology can still read the content even though it's been secured. To demonstrate this I have the 01 08 file open on my computer, and what we're going to do is come up here to our "file" menu and we're going to go to "properties". And up at the top, if you click on the "security" tab this is where we can apply security to our documents. Now, the security method, if you click on the drop-down menu, there's a number of ways in which you can assign security, some of which require you to have specific software that would allow you to do that. That would apply to certificate security, AEM security, and [Microsoft](../../Instructors/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Microsoft.md) Purview. To demonstrate this concept to you we're going to use the simplest method which is password security. Now when I choose that, the password security setting's dialogue box is going to open up and allow me to determine how I want to secure this document. Okay, now at this point I haven't done anything, okay? But if I click on this check box right here this is going to, in some way, restrict what I'm allowed to do in this document. >
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/security-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=95)** by default printing is not allowed, but if you want to allow printing you can choose high or low resolution. And then the second option is "do you want to allow any changes". Now typically you would set this to none, because if you're securing the document you're doing it for a purpose, and you don't want somebody to be able to just come in this file and make edits to the file without you knowing about it. The options you choose here are not particularly relevant they're going to be specific to your situation. But what I want to point out to you is this guy right down here, okay? And the good news is is that it's enabled by default. The checkbox is "enable text access for screen reader devices for the visually impaired". And by checking this check box you're basically saying "yes I've secured this document but allow access to the text to at least allow screen reader users to read the text so they can read the document." Now the one other thing I'm going to mention here when you're applying security to a file it's really easy to just say "hey, no changes allowed at all." The one exception that you want to be aware of is if you're ever applying security to a PDF form. Because if you say "changes allowed, none" the form fields are going to be disabled
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/security-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=189)** and nobody's going to be able to fill out the form fields and that, of course, is not a good situation in the case of a PDF form. So I want to point out an option for you to be aware of if you are applying security to a form. If you click on the "changes allowed" dropdown menu you're going to notice that there's an option in there for filling in form fields and signing signature fields. For a fillable PDF form that's the option you need to enable that will still restrict editing of the document but allow users to fill in the form fields that you've added to the PDF, okay? Now this particular document I'm not doing that so I am going to change it back to "none", and I'm going to click "OK". I do need to give it a password to restrict editing of the document. So down here where it says "change permissions password" I'm going to go ahead and type a password, okay? And then when I click "OK" I'm going to type it again. And I know I kind of blasted through that first dialogue box. What I'm actually going to do... let's kind of go back in there. Set that to "no security" Go back to oops... Go back to "password security", okay. So we had said "high resolutions" then we'll enter a password. I want to point out this dialogue box that pops up.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/security-and-accessibility?u=76281980&t=284)** Keep in mind when you apply security to this file it's not the most secure thing in the world - it's actually far from it - so just remember that even though you're applying security to this document it can be thwarted if somebody really wants to get in. I would say for the majority of people if they open up a PDF and it says "this file is secured" they're probably going to stop and say "oh boy, I can't access that." If somebody really wants to get in, however, this can be thwarted, okay? So this dialogue box says "all Adobe applications honor these restriction settings but not all third party [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) may honor it." Once I click "OK" you'll notice up here in the title bar it does say that this document is secured, right? So if I do a "save as" on this... And I'll call this my finished version... What you'll find is that if somebody does come in here and say "edit PDF" right away... Again, because Acrobat is an Adobe product it's honoring those security settings. So it's basically saying, "hey you can't edit this unless you have the password." Again, it can be thwarted, and if somebody really wants to get in they certainly can. So you can safely apply security to documents and keep them accessible, you just need to be sure you enable the appropriate settings so users of assistive technology can still read the content.

#### [Tagging challenging elements](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tagging-challenging-elements?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tagging-challenging-elements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes despite our best efforts, some elements just don't output as we expect them to. One example of this is placing PDF files in InDesign. They just output as figures, even though they may contain valuable information including text. Another example is [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) art in [Microsoft Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md). > **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tagging-challenging-elements?u=76281980&t=97)** there is in fact text to be utilized right? > **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tagging-challenging-elements?u=76281980&t=283)** Now, this is not mandatory, but what I'm going to do, I just like to be clean, you know, as I'm working here. So I'll select all these P tags, move them into the document tag so that they're nested appropriately. And the difference here is that now we are making it so much easier for a user to navigate this content. They can read this information paragraph by paragraph. If they want to go back, they can go back and read the previous one and then proceed to the next one. We're giving them control over how this content is being read. >
> **[7:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tagging-challenging-elements?u=76281980&t=467)** you could take the extra steps to improve on the situation. It's just going to add time to the process.

#### [Using Autotag](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/using-autotag?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/using-autotag?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A lot of people I talk to hear that Acrobat has an autotag feature, and think they've found the Holy Grail of PDF accessibility. #### [Fixing decorative bullets and numbers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-decorative-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-decorative-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's really common for designers and content creators to create unique bullets or numbers in a design to achieve a specific look. The challenge, however, is that it causes content to be tagged incorrectly. And as we learned earlier in this course, that violates WCAG Success Criterion 1.3.1, Info and Relationships. When bullets or numbers are created manually, the source application doesn't know how to properly tag them and often results in an inappropriate tagging structure. I have a document open here, and you can see on the left side here the designer has created these unique numbered items that they wanted to apply to the text to the right to indicate more of a numbered list. Now, what I wanted to show you is I have the same document open here in InDesign, and you can see over here on the right is the text, and then they created these manual circles for each one of the numbers. And you could see here, these are just shapes that I can adjust, I can manipulate, I can do whatever I want to them, okay? Now, the problem that that creates in Acrobat, if we dive into the tag structure here, you will see that as I get down to those items... There we go. You can see that each one of these items is tagged as a P tag, okay? Well, again, we learned earlier in this course that that violates Info and Relationships, because if it's a list, it should be tagged as a list. Now, it gets even better,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-decorative-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980&t=94)** because if you open up the P tag, because of how this was created in Adobe InDesign, each one of those circles with a number was its own text frame, okay? And by doing that, it essentially outputs that item in a section tag, right? And if you look here, you could see that here's my section tag and inside of it is a P tag with the number 1. So it's absolutely not tagged correctly, and what I'm going to do, just to save a little bit of time, I'm going to open each one of these just so we can see them, okay? Now, the good news is, is that I have everything I need to work with. I'm going to click on this first P tag, which is the number 1, and then I'm going to Ctrl-Click on the second, third, and fourth one. So you can see I have all the numbers are currently selected. Now, what I'm going to do is I'm going to right-click on any of those selected tags, I'm going to choose Properties, and I'm going to change the type here. I'm going to change that to a label. Okay? Now, by doing that, fundamentally, this is now tagged correctly, and the only thing I'm going to going to do is I'm going to grab each one of these labels and pull them out of the section so that it's the first thing
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-decorative-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980&t=187)** in each one of these container P tags, okay? Then what I'm going to do is I'm going to select all of those empty section tags, I'm going to right-click and choose Properties, and change them to List Item Body. And now what I'm going to do is I'm going to drag all of the text elements and put them into the L bodies. And then the P tags themselves, I'm going to highlight them and change them to list items. And so I leveraged all the containers that I had at my disposal to properly structure these list items the way they need to be. And all I have to do to finish this up is create one more new tag for the container list and then move those list items into that list tag. So now this is properly structured as a list. It adheres to Info and Relationships because the list is tagged as a list. And when this is read, assistive technology is going to say "list with four items", and it's going to properly tell the user that they're reading a list and there's four items in that list. So a really great way to kind of leverage what we have
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-decorative-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980&t=282)** when those decorative numbers or bullets are used. It's not the end of the world. It just means that you have a little bit of cleanup to do to get them to be structured the way that they need to be. So if you need to create a custom look for bullets or numbers, just understand that you'll need to put additional work in after the PDF file is created so that it is read appropriately by assistive technology.

So as you can see a seemingly harmless edit can have disastrous results when editing a PDF. If you have the source file, make the edit there, but avoid editing in Acrobat whenever possible.

### 2. Working with Tables in Adobe Acrobat

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Defining header cells in a table](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/defining-header-cells-in-a-table?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/defining-header-cells-in-a-table?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] WCAG states that it is a failure if a table does not contain header cells to make the headers programmatically determinable. Now that's sort of a fancy way of saying that tables need header cells to define the category for the data cells. In this video, we'll go through a few methods of defining header cells in a table. So I'm starting here with the 0201 document open on my computer. And if I open up the tags pane, you could see right here I have a table that is being used to tag the content of this table on the page. Now if I open up the table and I open up the first row, which I would call a header row, you could see that the cells within that row are simply defined as table data cells. So that alone is a failure because visually, that information at the top of the table is the header row of the table but it is not tagged accordingly. Now one thing you could do that's a pretty simple fix is we could select these two data cells, we can right click on them and choose properties. And in the type dropdown menu, we could choose table header cell. Now that does accomplish the task of defining those cells as header cells, but that method falls a bit short because in addition to defining the cells as headers, we also need to define the scope of those header cells. The scope is defined as either a column, a row, or both. And in this case, the row at the top of the table
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/defining-header-cells-in-a-table?u=76281980&t=96)** should be defined as a column header. So to take that step, what we're going to do is come over here to our accessibility tool inside of Adobe Acrobat and we're going to click on the reading order button. Now I want to point something out for those of you who may have just installed Adobe Acrobat on your computer. Oftentimes, this checkbox down at the bottom that says display like elements in a single block is enabled. I like to disable it because I like the way it displays the contents of the table. Now getting back to our header cells, I'm going to click on any one of the labels found within this table. And when you do that, you're going to notice that the table editor button becomes available. So I'm going to click on the table editor button. Now the table editor is roughly replicating how the table looks in my document. And what we could do is I'm going to click on the first cell of the header row and then I'm going to shift click on the second cell. And now if you right click on either of those selected cells, we could choose table cell properties. Now you'll notice these cells are already defined as a header cell, but right here is the scope dropdown menu that is currently specified as none. And I could tell you, this will cause a failure when you're checking this PDF using a checker such as the PAC 2021 checker
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/defining-header-cells-in-a-table?u=76281980&t=193)** or the CommonLook validator. So in the scope dropdown, I'm going to choose column to tell these header cells that they are column headers in the table. And now when I click okay, the table editor is replicating and showing me how this table is structured. Now one of the things you'll notice, it's really kind of hard to tell that are header cells. They really don't look much different. And so what we could do is if you right click on this table and choose table editor options, we have the ability to change how the table editor displays different elements of the table. And you can see that right now my header cell color is defined as red, which isn't showing up very well against that green background. So if you wish, you could click on the color next to header cell and I could choose something a little more drastic. I wonder if I choose, say, a blue color and click okay, now you could see that header cell is a little more prominent and it allows me to see the cells that are defined as header cells within this table. And you'll notice it now, if I were to run the Acrobat checker, so I can run the accessibility check here, you'll notice that there are no failures on this table, especially in the category of the table. I'm going to go ahead and do a save as here and I'm going to call this 0201, finish.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/defining-header-cells-in-a-table?u=76281980&t=290)** And what I can also do is run that document through the PAC checker and you can see that there are no failures. The only failure I have right now is a [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md) failure because I did not add a title to this document. So the table elements of that document are completely passing with flying colors. So choose whichever method you like but do make sure that you define header cells as well as scope in your tables.

#### [Tables that span multiple pages](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tables-that-span-multiple-pages?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tables-that-span-multiple-pages?u=76281980&t=0)** - When tables span multiple pages, problems can occur regarding the accessibility level of those documents. Let's take a look at an example, and I'll walk you through some considerations to keep in mind when dealing with tables that span multiple pages. Now, beginning here with the 02_02 document open, and this contains a table that has a list of baking substitutions, right? So, certainly useful information for our cookbook, but it's a table that starts on the left hand page and continues on the right hand page. Now that's all well and good. There's some things that are happening in this table that are beneficial for sighted users, right? One of which is the table head row, that the header row, is repeated on concurrent pages. And that's really helpful. If you think of the context of somebody flipping through a book, when they get to the next page, if the header's missing, they're going to be like, oh, well, what's the category of this information? Right? So, that's where the repeating header is beneficial. However, for somebody using assistive technology that can actually be disruptive, because, keep in mind, you know, users of assistive technology are going to read a single table, and if they're navigating down the rows, and all of a sudden the header is repeated, it really is not in a logical position that makes sense, because this first header is going to be a header for all of the data in the table, and the table knows that.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tables-that-span-multiple-pages?u=76281980&t=96)** So, let's take a look at what we've been given in the output of this table. So if we open up our tags pane, and we open up our table, this is a really common situation. Now, this particular file was created in Adobe InDesign, and this is a really common thing that we run into with output from Adobe InDesign. And what I want to show you, you'll notice we have a THead tag, and that's for the header of the table, and then we have a TBody, but note that the TBody is only for the body on the first page, and then you'll notice that the head is repeated, okay? And then so is the TBody. So, again, this creates a bit of a problem, because, functionally, this should be one table with one THead and one TBody, right? So, the fact that the header is repeated, that would be a disruptive experience, okay? So this second THead, although we have it visually, for the benefit of sighted users, it's not going to be helpful for users of assistive technology. So, what I'm going to do here is I'm going to open up the cells of that THead, and I'm going to select the marked content inside of there, right? So this is the actual text that is tagged, and I'm simply going to right click and choose "Change Tag to Artifact," and you can leave the setting at the default of a page artifact, and click okay. And now you'll notice that all of these cells are empty.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tables-that-span-multiple-pages?u=76281980&t=191)** So if I click on the THead, nothing is highlighted, so I can literally just hit delete and get rid of that. And then for proper table structure, what I'm going to do is move all of the rows from the second TBody, right? So I'm going to grab all those rows and move them up into the first TBody. And now that this TBody is empty, I can delete that as well. And so if you look at what we have now, we have one THead, we have one TBody, and we have one TFoot, right? And that's going to create a much more logical and much better experience for the user of assistive technology, because, even if they're reading, let me just go down to say, here, right? Even if they're reading this cell, it knows that "Use This" is the header for that cell, okay? And that's critical. We want to keep that in mind. And what we just did is we restructured the table so that it's reading in a much more logical way, and it'll be a lot easier for users of AT to interpret. Now, one other thing I'd like to bring up. Adobe InDesign does not allow you to do this, but [Microsoft Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) allows a row to break across a page. Imagine this "Molasses" row
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/tables-that-span-multiple-pages?u=76281980&t=286)** that has more information, and so the row is forced to continue over here. And that creates a really interesting situation, because [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) actually creates a new row for the continuation of the row from the first page. So, in a situation like that, again, that's going to create a very odd experience, because part of the "Molasses" row is going to be read, and then the back half of the row is going to be read on the next page. So, again, to fix that, what you want to do is combine that information into a single row. That way, that information will be interpreted correctly. So, we can't completely avoid tables that span multiple pages. It's something that we just need to deal with. But understanding that some additional work will be required to make those documents fully accessible, is good information to remember when you're building your documents.

#### [Understanding table spans](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/understanding-table-spans?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/understanding-table-spans?u=76281980&t=0)** - Table spans occur whenever you merge cells in a table in the source application. It's a common practice to merge cells in a table for a variety of different reasons. But when you do this, you could end up with an accessibility issue if you don't properly remediate those tables. In order to be able to do this, you need to understand what a span is and how they're defined. So let's take a closer look. Here, we have a table that I've created and within this table I've created a couple of merged cells for the category of tubers as well as the category for vegetables. Now, this is what happens when we merge more than one cell together. When you go to check your document, you'll typically get what's called irregularity error. And irregularity error occurs whenever your table does not have the same number of cells in a row or a column. Okay? So, in this particular example here what used to be two cells, right, for tubers and for vegetables, have now been merged into one. So, you know, originally the tubers cell was this wide, right? And same thing for vegetables. But what we did is we merged them together. So now, we only have one. So this row has one cell, most of the other ones have two and that's what is going to cause irregularity error. Now, when we go on from here, what we need to remember is that in order to make this table compliant,
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/understanding-table-spans?u=76281980&t=97)** we need to add a span to that cell, okay? And a span can be in the form of a column span, a row span, or both, in which case both the columns and the rows have been merged. In the example I'm showing you here, these two cells would require what's called a column span, right? And so, the tubers and the vegetable cells that used to be two cells are now one, but they are spanning the equivalent of two cells. And because right now we're dealing with rows, we would need to apply a column span of two to both of these cells. Now, the way that you determine what number you use for a column span is you look at the other rows in the table and you really have to calculate which row has the most number of cells, right? Now, this table is really simple because all of the other rows have two cells. And so, in order for us to properly remediate this table, we would need to set the tuber cell and the vegetable cell to have a call span of one or a column span of one. Now, the same thing can happen sometimes when you merge multiple rows together. Like for example, let's say we wanted to merge potatoes and turnips together, okay? Now, in that case, we would need to apply a row span of two
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/understanding-table-spans?u=76281980&t=193)** because we have one cell that is spanning to rows, okay? So, once you understand how table spans work, you have the tools that you need to properly remediate tables within an accessible PDF file.

#### [Dealing with blank cells](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/dealing-with-blank-cells?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/dealing-with-blank-cells?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the questions I get asked all the time is, are blank cells okay in a table? And my response is, well, blank cells are okay for a sighted user. They're also okay for users of assistive technology. If a cell is blank, it effectively means there's no pertinent information to go into that spot. So basically, yes, blank cells are okay. Does that mean there's not a better approach? No. So let's talk about this in a little bit more detail. So I have the 0206 file open in front of me. And guys, if you haven't been following along with me up to this point I highly encourage you to open the exercise files for each video as I go through them so you can follow along. I want you to get as much out of these videos as possible. Now, I kind of purposefully modified this table to kind of illustrate a concept. And this is something I see a lot. You'll notice within the table, blackberries, cherries, fruit juices, huckleberries they have no information under blanching, right? And when a user is navigating through the table they're going to move to the right, into an empty cell, and there's going to be nothing to be read, right? So they're going to know that it's empty. I would much rather see you put in there, not applicable, or not recommended, or something like that to make it very, very clear to not just the user of assistive technology, but also to a sighted user. I think it's somewhat confusing.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/dealing-with-blank-cells?u=76281980&t=92)** You know, as soon as I see that blank cell my question is, well, should I not blanch blackberries, or should I not blanch huckleberries? So I think the more information, the better. The other thing I'm illustrating here is I often see what I call the A1 cell being blank. And if you look up here at the top of this, that cell is blank. Now, although a blank cell is okay, a blank header cell is not. Because that header cell is very important, as we know, as we've learned so far. The header cell defines the category for the data underneath that header. And this information over here has a category. There is a category that I can assign to that. Now, if you switch over to the B file. I've done exactly what I just talked about, right? You, you know, you may receive some challenges. What I want to show you is one solution that I do on
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/dealing-with-blank-cells?u=76281980&t=186)** a regular basis when I do have that client that is insistent that they don't want a category in this cell, okay? And what I do is I'll go into edit pdf and I'm going to come up here where it says add text. And I'm going to click in this area here and I'm literally going to type the word fruit, okay? Now I'm going to [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in so you can see this a little bit better. And if you're in the edit mode here what I could do is I can click on this. I can then right click and I could choose a range, send to back. Okay? So visually, I'm still satisfying my client's request that the A1 cell should be blank and should not have any content. However, what I'm going to do is I'm going to go to my accessibility tool and go into the reading order and let me zoom in again to make this a little bit easier to see here. And if I marquee this area that text that I created is there. And I'm going to tag that as a cell. Now, what that does for me structurally is that if I go into my tags pane what I need to do now is find out where it went. Here it is, it ended up all the way to the top here. So this is very common. Sometimes you have to kind of figure out where that tag went. But I'm going to open up the row that this top row here
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/dealing-with-blank-cells?u=76281980&t=281)** and I'm just going to slide that cell into position there. And what I'm going to want to do is change that to a a table header cell and I'll right click and go to properties, edit the attribute objects. I'm going to say new, change this to a table. I want to make sure that I add the scope to this. I'm going to say scope column, oops column. And I think the other cells already have that. I'm just going to double check. There we go. Yeah. So what I've done effectively is I've created a category for somebody using assistive technology so that when they read that cell, it's going to say fruit. And then they know that the information below there is in the category of fruit. So yes, blank cells are okay, blank header cells, not so much. I hope you now have a better understanding of how to work with blank cells in a table now and you'll be able to put it into practice in your own tables, in your own documents.

#### [What makes a bad table?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In order for us to create good tables, both visually and accessibly, we need to understand what exactly makes a bad table. Common practices that some of us have been using for years, or even decades, may find not to be the ideal approach for accurately conveying information. Let's take a closer look at what makes a bad table. So I'm beginning this video with one of my documents already open. This is the 02_07 file. And what I wanted to show you here, this is similar to one that we looked at previously where we have a couple of blank cells, okay? Now again, blank cells are allowed. However, we definitely could do better because having some information in there that is descriptive and gets the point across can be much more helpful than reading a completely blank cell, okay? Now, one approach that people take, I'm going to open up the B file now, so what they did in this table, which is one approach, is that you'll notice that they put an asterisk in these cells, okay? Now, one of the things I always try to convey to people, as a sighted user, we benefit from the helicopter view. And what I mean by that is that we can see many elements at a glance on the page, right?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980&t=97)** And as I'm looking at this table, when I see that asterisk, I'm immediately thinking, well, what does that mean? And as a sighted user, I can look to the bottom of this table and I see, oh, an asterisk means not recommended. Now, the problem for somebody using assistive technology is that they're not going to know what it means until they get to the bottom of the table. Now, one approach to addressing that is you could take that tagged content and structurally have it read before the table. And that way at least the user knows, oh, this character means not recommended. And then as they're reading the information in the table, they know what that means. Now, what I'd like to do is read this table using a screen reader.

>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980&t=194)** - [Instructor] So it does read star not recommended. But again, the experience is not an ideal one.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980&t=202)** - [Screen Reader] Exit. OK button.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980&t=204)** - [Instructor] There's another type of table you may have seen called a measles chart where they use a colored circle within the table to indicate meaning. That's another huge challenge because they may have created that circle using a font, but likely the character that's used for that font could be the letter Q. And so when somebody's reading the table, it's going to say Q. And you're like, well, what does that mean? So at a very, very least, we have to take extra steps to make sure that that is read appropriately. Now, in a previous video I showed you when we did the crate tag from selection video, if we wanted to, we could go into the tag structure and add actual text to that star and we could add actual text that says not recommended. Now, that would be a much better experience. Another thing I see a lot is they'll use like a check mark in the table indicating yes or no. Again, that font for a check mark could be the letter O. I mean, it could be some random character if it reads it at all. So what I want you to take away from this is that when you use those elements, at the very, very least, you have extra work that you're going to have to do to make the experience a good one. Now, I have one more document I wanted to show you because an easy fix for this problem
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980&t=298)** is to simply put not recommended in the cell. I mean, you can't get any clearer than this. Now again, some clients, and again, you have them, I have them, they may vehemently argue against this. They may not like the way it looks. They may like the tiny asterisk to indicate that it's not recommended, but this approach that we're looking at here is a very good solution because it leaves nothing to question. The value of that cell is going to say not recommended and nobody's going to question what it means. Nobody's going to have to do any extra work. It's going to be a very good experience for the user. I hope you found this video helpful. I highly encourage you guys, take some time and evaluate your own tables and try to take a look at instances where you're using a character or a symbol to indicate meaning and see if you can come up with a better solution. And again, at the end of the day, we can always make those characters work, but we're going to have to put time and effort into making it happen. A lot of times, it's just much easier to use instead of a green check mark, use the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) approved. If you use a red check mark, simply use the text not approved, right? I know you're removing a little bit of the design element,
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/what-makes-a-bad-table?u=76281980&t=395)** but again, if the design element is critical, then you may have to resort to using actual text so that when a user's reading those values, they're going to get the exact meaning that you're going for.

#### [Creative table solutions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creative-table-solutions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creative-table-solutions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes solutions to challenging problems can be staring you in the face but you fail to see them. One of the things that I love about PDF accessibility is the fact that you can modify the underlying tag structure without visually affecting the document. In this video, I'd like to show you a creative solution to a problem that may help you with your own documents and provide a really great experience to users of assistive technology. So I'm starting here with this advertising [Statistics](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Statistics.md) bar chart open on my screen. And if I open my tags pane and I look at my tags, you can see, sure enough, this bar chart is currently tagged as a figure. When a bar chart's tagged as a figure, your only recourse is to describe it using alternate text. And if I look at the property of this figure, you can see that I have in fact described this bar chart but it's kind of ambiguous. There's just a ton of text here that's going to be read aloud and it's going to be really hard for a user to process that and really retain it. And so it's really not a great experience, although it will be compliant, right? I mean, the only requirement for a figure is that we have alternate text but whenever I have this situation I always try to err on the side of the user and I want the user to have a good experience.

#### [Creating heading and data associations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-heading-and-data-associations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-heading-and-data-associations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Lecturer] When working with complex tables it's necessary to create heading and data cell associations. This is because when you create a header cell in a table anywhere other than the top or left side, assistive technology has no idea how those header rows are supposed to behave. In this video I'm going to show you the process. And to be fair, it's a bit arduous. But you'll know how it's done. So I'm starting this video with the 02_09 document open on my screen. And this is a classic example of a table where somebody created these, what I would call subheader cells, to kind of categorize the cells into tubers and vegetables. Right? And again, you know you're going to hear me say it again. I'm always an advocate of simplifying your tables because doing this does take some work. But. So what I'm going to do, I'm going to open the tags pane, and I'm going to open up this table and we could see the top row. They need to be header cells, right? So I'm going to go ahead and change them to table header cells. And then in addition, if we go down to the next row. The first cell in there also needs to be a table header cell. Okay? And while we're here I'm going to delete that empty cell that's in there. And then let's go down to vegetables. We'll delete that empty cell, and we'll change this one.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-heading-and-data-associations?u=76281980&t=93)** I'll just type this one in manually. There we go. Now I'm going to go ahead and do a save as on this. And I'm going to call this 02_09_finish, right? And then what I'm going to do is, I'm going to go ahead and open up the table editor. So if we go to our accessibility tool, go to reading order. I'm going to click on any one of these labels and click the table editor button. The tubers cell, I'm going to right click and choose table cell properties. And we need to apply a column span of 2 to that cell. Let's go ahead and click OK. And then same thing for vegetables. We need that to be a column span of 2. And that's going to properly define the span for that cell. Okay? To define the header cell association, the table editor automates a little bit of it (laughs). And it is helpful, right? But the way we do that, if you right click there's an option down here that says Auto Generate Header Cell IDs. Okay? And I'm going to go ahead and choose that option. You're not going to visually see anything right now. However if you click on any of the header cells, and you right click and choose table cell properties, you'll notice that it automatically created
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-heading-and-data-associations?u=76281980&t=187)** a header cell ID for the header. Okay? And you'll find that in any of the ones that we click on here. If I click on vegetables you're going to see the same thing. Right? What we now need to do, so for example I'm going to click on tubers, I'm going to right click and choose table cell properties. And I need to tell it that vegetable and cooking time are headers for the tuber cell. Okay? So I'm going to click the plus sign, and then it gives me a dropdown. So I'm going to choose the R1C1_Vegetabl. Click OK. So now you can see that's been added. And then I'm going to add again, and I'm going to choose the cooking cell as well. So what I did, is vegetable and cooking time are now header cells associated to tubers. Okay? Let's click on potatoes. I'm going to right click table cell properties. Now what I need to do here, is I need to add the R1C1_Vegetabl. I need to add the R1C2_Cooking, and I also need to add the R2C1_Tubers. Okay? Now all of those cells are associated with this one cell. So now vegetable. Actually I made a mistake on this one. Cooking is not a header of potatoes,
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/creating-heading-and-data-associations?u=76281980&t=281)** so only vegetable and tubers. Okay? And then I would click on boil, and what I would do here is hit the plus sign. We're going to add the R1C2_Cooking, and then we're also going to add the tubers. So that now tubers and cooking time are headers for that cell. Okay? Now guys I am not going to continue on from here, because as you can see it's just going to take way too long for me to complete this. What I will do, is I will update this finish file for you, so that you can open it up and check it out for yourself. And you can see how it looks when all of the header cell IDs have been associated. As I said, this process is arduous. I don't do this in Acrobat anymore because of how long it takes. Instead I use a product called axesPDF, which lets me define header cell associations in seconds. Still I think it's valuable to understand how it's done. And if nothing else will encourage you to keep your tables simple, in documents that you create in the future.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **UI Navigation:** click on (7), go to (2), open the (1), dropdown (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (1)

#### [Linearizing tables](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/linearizing-tables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/linearizing-tables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are times when elements that are built as a table shouldn't be a table at all. Tables typically are used to display data in a logical way, but sometimes designers and content creators will use tables for formatting reasons only. In cases like these, we can change the structure of those elements so that the content is not contained in a table and is read in a more appropriate way. This process is called linearizing a table and I'd like to show you that here with this document open. So when we look at this document, at a glance, you wouldn't think that any of these elements would be a table at all. We have some paragraphs, we have a bunch of paragraphs over here but diving a little bit deeper into the tag structure reveals that we do in fact have a table and if we click on it, you could see that the ingredients for the angel cake has been organized into a table. Now again, a lot of times designers and content creators do this because it helps with formatting. Structurally, it makes no sense at all and as a matter of fact, it's somewhat of a barrier because you're forcing the user to enter into a table and navigate all of the rows, where realistically, they're really just a bunch of paragraphs. So to avoid this problem or to alleviate this problem, we could do something called linearizing the table and linearizing the table essentially means
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/linearizing-tables?u=76281980&t=96)** you take the content of the table and pull it out of the table structure and just have it read as text. What we could do here is if we open up our table, we could see that this table is comprised of about eight rows, but if we open up each row and each cell, you're going to see that there's a paragraph within each cell and that's the text element within the cell. Now, occasionally you may run into a table where within the cell all you have is marked content, so all you have are these little container elements and that's okay too. In that situation, what I would do is I would highlight all of the TDs and change them to P tags, right? But in our case because each TD contains a paragraph, I'm going to grab those paragraphs. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to select all those P tags and all I'm going to do is drag all of those selected tags up and out of the table. So if I drag this up and out, when I let go, now you can see all of those P tags are simply being read in the correct order but they're no longer inside of a table and when you click on this table, you could see that it's empty because nothing on the page is being highlighted. I can literally just delete that table and now you'll see that if you walk your tags tree,
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/linearizing-tables?u=76281980&t=190)** we go from our heading to a paragraph to another heading and then we read all of the ingredients, jump to the next heading, paragraph, heading, paragraph. So what this does, what this achieves is you've simplified the tag structure, you're now making it easier for users of assistive technology to read the content. They're not having to dive into a table unnecessarily to read the content. So in cases when you have a table element that really shouldn't be a table, linearize it to improve the reading experience for users of assistive technology.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **UI Navigation:** click on (2)
> **Definitions:** is called (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Adding a tooltip to form fields](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-a-tooltip-to-form-fields?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-a-tooltip-to-form-fields?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In order to make form fields in a PDF file accessible, one of the key requirements is to add a tooltip or description to each form. Because form field names are not read by assistive technology, we need a tooltip to provide a description to the user that explains what they're expected to enter into the field. Let me show you how this is done. So I'm going to go back to the Prepare Form mode and that is going to show me all of the form fields I have in this form. Now, one of the things I want to point out, you'll notice that all of your form fields are listed down here in the lower right when you're in Prepare Form mode. What's useful to know is that this is also the tab order that will be used when somebody is tabbing from field to field in this form. So you may notice here, because of the order in which I created these fields, currently it's going from the city to the zip and then to the state. Now what I could do is just drag the state up and position it in between the city and the zip. So now it goes city, state, zip, and I can just arrow down and just make sure that it's reading these fields in the order that I want them to and this looks pretty good. Now, if I start with the first form field, I'm just going to [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in a little bit to make this easier to see. If I double click on the first name field and go to the general tab.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-a-tooltip-to-form-fields?u=76281980&t=93)** Of course, we have the name of our field that we entered previously but right here is your tooltip. Now to kind of show you the reasoning behind this, if I go to my accessibility tool and run the accessibility check, you're going to notice that I got a bunch of errors under the category of [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md). And one is that the form fields are not tagged. We're going to cover that in a future video, but the other error we're getting is that all of these fields, the field descriptions, failed. And Acrobat uses multiple terms to describe the same thing. Field descriptions is also the tooltip, okay? So if I go back to Prepare Form, I'll double click on the first name field. And in the tooltip, the recommendation for the tooltip is, we all have a tendency to want to say, "please enter your first name here." You could kind of save the pleasantries and just kind of be to the point, like here, I would just say, "enter your legal first name here," like nothing fancy. And then here I would say, "enter your legal last name here," right? You're just basically describing what the user is expected to enter in here. So you want to kind of go through. For the state, remember we made that a dropdown menu. Here we can say, "choose your state from the list," right?
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-a-tooltip-to-form-fields?u=76281980&t=189)** And you want to pay attention to the type of field it is, because for a dropdown menu, you wouldn't say, "enter your state here," so. "Enter your zip code." And then finally, "enter your email address." And then every form field needs to have a tooltip. For the check boxes, I would do something like, "choose if you are interested in," and then what I'm going to do is I'm going to copy this to save some time, and I'll type "landscape design." And then when I go to the next field, I can just paste that in and say, "landscaping contracting." You kind of get the idea. Here we go. And then when we get down to the radio buttons, so let's see, "who will be performing the installation?" What we can do is just say, "choose who will be performing the installation." And with the radio buttons, this is one of the situations where it's a little unique because all of the radio buttons, because they all have the same name, they're all going to automatically have the same tooltip. And so, remember the option is what makes each one unique, okay? And then finally, this field down here,
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-a-tooltip-to-form-fields?u=76281980&t=285)** I would just say, "tell us anything else about the project that we should know." As we move on here, you're going to notice that there is some redundancy that is built in but I'll explain to you why we're doing that, right? Because when you think about it, you know when this form is being read, it's going to say "first name" and then they're going to get to the first name field. Then it's going to say "address" and they're going to get to the address field. So we'll talk about that a little bit later. But now, if I run my accessibility check again, you're going to notice that in the field descriptions category, everything is now passing. Because we appropriately added the tooltip to all of the form fields and we're satisfying that requirement. So as you can see, tooltips are required and it improves the experience for users of assistive technology. So make sure to add a tooltip for each form field in your document for maximum compliance.

#### [Manually tagging form fields](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/manually-tagging-form-fields?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/manually-tagging-form-fields?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Another accessibility requirement of Form Fields is that they need to be tagged. I can't explain why Acrobat is incapable of doing this for us automatically, but it is what it is. There are two ways to tag Form Fields. And in this video I'll show you how to tag them manually using the Reading Order Panel. I'm starting this video with the 0303 document already open. And to kind of show you the problem, I'm going to go ahead and go to the Accessibility Tool, and I'm going to run the Accessibility Check. And I know I've said before that the Acrobat Checker does not check to any known standard, and that is correct. >
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/manually-tagging-form-fields?u=76281980&t=188)** the items selected that you want. There we go. (mouse clicking) Two and three. And then finally we have this big one down here for our notes. (mouse clicking) And I'm going to go ahead and tag that. (mouse clicking) If we rerun our Accessibility Check, you'll now see that "Tagged Form Fields" is totally passing, no problems whatsoever. And if you open your Tags Pane you're now going to see that you actually have a Formed Tag for every single Form Field in your document. So, we've achieved the objective. We've tagged all of our Form Fields. So, by leveraging the Reading Order Panel, you can appropriately tag all of your Form Fields getting you that much closer to a compliant document.
