#### [Automatically tagging form fields](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/automatically-tagging-form-fields?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/automatically-tagging-form-fields?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An alternate approach to tagging form fields is to do it automatically. This approach is a bit faster than tagging form fields manually and of course saves a bit of time. Let me show you how this method works. What I'm going to do is I'm going to come over here to my tags pane, and similar to what we did before I'm going to click on the tags icon way up here at the top. The purpose of this is to simply make sure that all my form tags fall into one location and don't get buried underneath another tag. And then what I'm going to do is I'm going to click on the option button at the top of the tags pane and I'm going to choose Find. Now in the Find element dialogue, if you click on the dropdown menu, there's about six different items that we can find from in here. And what I'm going to do is I'm going to choose Unmarked Annotations, okay? And I'm going to search the entire document, even though this is only a one page document I just want to search the entire document. And I'm going to hit the Find button. And what you're going to notice is that it finds the first form field in your document. With that field found, you can click on the Tag element button and it asks you how you would like to tag this element. Well, we know that we want to tag this as a form, so I'm going to choose that from the dropdown menu and then I'm going to click the okay button. And what it'll do is it'll automatically tag that form field
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/automatically-tagging-form-fields?u=76281980&t=96)** and then it'll find the next form field in your document. So what you can do to kind of speed up this process is click the tag element button with your mouse, and then just hit the Enter key on your keyboard. And I'm just going to kind of keep repeating that until I get through all of my form fields. When it's tagged all of them, you're going to get a dialogue that says, find completed. And I'll go ahead and click okay. We can close this. And you'll notice in your tags pane, similar to what we had in the previous video, we have all of our form fields are tagged and located in the tags pane. And once again if we run the accessibility check, you're going to see that now under the [Forms](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Forms.md) category, there are no other errors remaining. You'll notice in here we do have a tab order failed. We could fix that a number of different ways, but probably the easiest way is to click on that error, right click on it and choose fix. And what that does is it sets your tab order to match your tag order, okay? That's basically all there is to it. So as you can see, by using the find command in the tags pane, you can automatically find and tag all of the forms in your document.

> **[8:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/structuring-form-tags-in-the-tags-panel?u=76281980&t=519)** - [Chad] Okay? So in forms mode, NVDA kind of ignores the tag structure and only focuses on the forms fields. And it's a different experience. And so a lot of people ask me, "Chad, why do I have to spend the time "to put the form tag nested "in the appropriate location within the tag structure?" And my answer is, because you and I can't control how a user chooses to read our document. There are plenty of users out there, just like there are users out there who use software who kind of utilize that software at a fairly basic level. They don't know about more advanced features. They don't know about some of the capabilities. And so, if a user is unaware of forms mode in NVDA, the default is browse mode. And so their experience is going to be... Let me switch back over
>
> **[9:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/structuring-form-tags-in-the-tags-panel?u=76281980&t=576)** - [Voice Assistant] Hardscaping. Checkbox not checked. Choose if you are interested in water features and garden structures. Water features and garden structures. Heading level three, who will be performing the installation? Choose one. Radio button not checked. Choose who will be performing the installation. Do it yourself. You will do it yourself. Radio button not checked. Choose who will be performing the installation. L and M Contractors, Leaf and Mortar's contractors.
>
> **[9:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/structuring-form-tags-in-the-tags-panel?u=76281980&t=598)** - [Chad] All right, so that's how it sounds in browse mode. So, again, we don't have the ability to control that. And WCAG states that our documents need to be able to be read by all technologies. It's important that we build our document to account for every situation that we can. And by nesting the tags appropriately, we're ensuring that regardless of whether a user uses browse mode or forms mode, they're going to have a good experience. So by taking the extra time to structure the form tags in the appropriate order, you're ensuring a good reading experience for users of assistive technology.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

I encourage you to leverage it whenever you need it to help you make your files as accessible as possible.

Now, what I find that's pretty common, people have a tendency to over bullet things. Because when somebody using assistive technology is reading a bulleted list, you know, it's going to say list with five items. And then when they read the first bullet, in the case of this example here, they'll read the first bullet and then they're going to hear again, list with three items
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-multilevel-lists?u=76281980&t=189)** because they're now in a sub list of the first list item. And so you can imagine if you have to keep drilling down into that list structure, it's kind of really easy to get lost and it can really be a bit complicated. So bullets are fine, numbers are fine. Just try to keep them fairly simple if it all possible. Now I'm going to close this. Oftentimes it's a lot longer than this.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-multilevel-lists?u=76281980&t=282)** Let me just make it a two line. There we go. That'll work. >
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-multilevel-lists?u=76281980&t=377)** into that first one and delete that empty list tag. So now you have that list still structured as a list the way it's supposed to be and that paragraph is just a child of the second list item. So I hope that helps you to understand the challenge that you'll run into when you do insert a paragraph like that, but then also how to fix it if you do in fact need to create that design. So when using multi-level lists, be sure to keep them in sequence and don't break them up using additional paragraphs. If you do, you'll just need to do a little bit of fixing in the PDF after export.

#### [Setting hyperlink tooltips](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/setting-hyperlink-tooltips?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/setting-hyperlink-tooltips?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the requirements of the PDF UA standard is that every hyperlink needs to contain a tool tip. Now, if you think this requirement is a bit overkill I should explain that this requirement came from a time when it was really common to use "Click here" or "Download here" as the text of a hyperlink. And using those terms doesn't tell the user anything about where they're going and that inherently was the problem. You know this is bad practice and you're probably careful now about making sure you use meaningful text for your hyperlinks, but regardless, it is still a requirement. Now, [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) actually allows you to meet this requirement. So I'd like to show you how it does that here. Now I'm starting this video with the 0406 document open and I've navigated to page nine where I'm on the category of [LinkedIn](../../Skills/Web%20Development/LinkedIn.md). And so what I'm going to do is I'm just going to type a sentence down here at the end of this paragraph that says for more information visit the LinkedIn Learning website.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/setting-hyperlink-tooltips?u=76281980&t=67)** How appropriate, right? And then what I'm going to do is I'm going to highlight LinkedIn Learning website. I'm going to right click and I'm going to choose link. And this is going to allow me to say, okay let's go to an existing webpage. This is the text to display up here, right? So it just kind of copies it from what I had selected. And then down here I could type the URL of the website I want it to go to. So I'm going to go www.[linkedin.com/learning](https://linkedin.com/learning). Okay? Now, if I want to add the tool tip in the upper right hand corner you'll notice there's a screen tip button. And if I click on that this is where I can add the tool tip. Now I know I'm using a couple different terminologies here. There are a number of terms that are used. So I use the term tool tip. Word is calling it a screen tip and the technical term that you may hear is called a contents entry. So in the screen tip text I'm going to type in here LinkedIn Learning website. Okay? And then I'll go ahead and click, okay. And so I'm going to click, okay now we have our hyperlink created. I'm going to do a save as on this and I'll call it the finished version. And then I'm going to create a PDF of this document. So I'll just leave that name as is, and I'll let
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/setting-hyperlink-tooltips?u=76281980&t=162)** the PDF maker convert this to the PDF. What I am going to do here, I'm going to open my pack checker. And so if I run the 0406 finished document through the checker, we are going to have some errors in this document, which is normal. If I go to the PDF, what I'll do I think that was on page 10, right? Yeah, there it is. And let me [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in on this and I'll just kind of show you where this is stored. Find that tag. Here we go. And here is the link, right? The LinkedIn Learning website. And if you wanted to see where that screen tip that we added is located
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/setting-hyperlink-tooltips?u=76281980&t=256)** if you right click on the link and choose properties, you'll notice in the alternate text field we have LinkedIn Learning website. That is where you know that screen tip is going to be found inside of your document. I'm just going to close that and just remember to maximize accessibility and compliance when creating a hyperlink, take the extra step and add the tool tip to the hyperlink as well.

They just work. The reason I'm mentioning it at all is because it's a common misconception that you're not allowed to merge cells in a table. That is not the case with InDesign. In [Microsoft Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md), sure, but InDesign handles merge cells amazingly and I'd like to show you that here in this document. So I'm beginning this video with the 0501 document open and I'm just going to [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in on this table on the right hand page of the spread and if you look here, the Tubers cell up here at the top, that is a merged cell, right, and if I go down a little bit further, the Vegetable cell, again, also a merged cell, okay, so if I export this to a pdf, which I'm going to do by going to the file menu in InDesign and choosing export and I'm going to choose PDF interactive and I'm going to go ahead and export this document to a pdf. All right. Now if I look at the tag structure here, I dive in here and I find my table, it's nested inside of this P tag here, there we go, what you'll find is that if you go to, in my case I'm going to go to the second row here, we have one cell in that row
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-merged-table-cells?u=76281980&t=97)** and then we also have that down here, right, we only have one cell, so normally, right, this would cause a regularity error because other applications fail to define what's called the column span. In InDesign, however, if I right click on this cell and go to properties and I look at the attribute objects, you're going to see that InDesign properly defines the column span whenever you merge cells and that applies to whether you merge columns or whether you merge rows. If we had merged rows, we would've a row span attribute in here and if I go to the other one, do the same thing here, edit attribute objects and you could see that this one also has a column span. So again, really nothing special you have to do, I just want you to know that you can do it. Just use the merge cell feature in InDesign and pretty much merge away. InDesign'll take it from there and make sure that your merge sells output correctly.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-table-headers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] As you know, every table needs to have a header in order to meet compliance. But just because it looks like a header row doesn't mean that it actually is, okay? And the way in which we can make sure that it is a header row is by grabbing our type tool. And if you click to the left of that header row, you will highlight that entire row. Now to convert it to a header, if you come up here to the table menu and choose Convert Rows, you'll have an option that says to header, okay? >

Because of that, I actually like using table styles because it reminds me when I forgot to convert a row to a header, right? Whenever possible, I try to take advantage of table styles in InDesign. So it starts on the left page and continues on the right page. Now, you can't edit this row, right? So I'll tell you what I'm going to do. I'm going to export this to a PDF and I'll just leave that name the same. And if I go to the tags tree here,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-table-headers?u=76281980&t=191)** there we go. So a couple of things about InDesign in general. Now, if you were to run that through the PAC Checker, you're going to get an error, right? Because you're going to get an error that says invalid use of a P structure element, or it might be a table structure element, one or the other, but it's basically just saying that this tag does not belong in this location. So what I typically do as a matter of practice is I'll just move the table out of the P tag and then delete that empty P tag just to alleviate that problem. But looking at the table, if we open this up, I've talked about this in previous videos, you'll notice that we have two T heads, two T bodies, and two T foots, okay? And as a matter of fact, we don't want the footer read the first time. So what I do to fix this is I'll open up the T body and then I'll open up the other T body and I'm just going to move all of these body rows into the first T body. And now that this T body is empty, we can delete it. The first T foot, we really don't want that to be read
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-table-headers?u=76281980&t=287)** because the bottom of the table is actually over here. So I'm going to open this up and I'm going to artifact this, right-click, change tag to artifact, click OK. And now because the T foot is empty, we can delete it. And then we're going to do the same thing with the T head. And this is three separate cells. So I need to open all of them, select the marked content, right-click and choose change tag to artifact. And now this second T head, because it's empty, we can delete it. And now you'll see that the table is simply structured as T head, T body, T foot, exactly the way the table is supposed to be structured. So I really don't have much advice as far as like avoiding that problem, although in a future video, I'm going to demonstrate a plugin for InDesign called MadeToTag. MadeToTag basically fixes this problem. If you do a lot of tables in InDesign, especially tables that span across a page, you may want to take a look at MadeToTag because it basically alleviates the need to fix all of your tables after you export. Anyway, as you can see, defining a header in InDesign is quick and easy. Although if you need to define row headers, you will need to do that inside of Adobe Acrobat.

#### [Getting creative with bullets and numbers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/getting-creative-with-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/getting-creative-with-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's really common in InDesign to get creative with bullets and numbers to create a specific effect. However, doing so can often result in a document that's not appropriately tagged. In this video, I'd like to show you some workarounds for dealing with creative bullets that don't output appropriately. Now, I'm starting this video with the 05_03 document open on my computer, and I want to focus on this numbered list over here on the right hand page. >
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/getting-creative-with-bullets-and-numbers?u=76281980&t=94)** you're going to get the error inappropriate use of a section structure element, and that's because a section tag doesn't belong inside of a paragraph. #### [Adding alt text for hyperlinks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-alt-text-for-hyperlinks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-alt-text-for-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the requirements of the PDF/UA standard is that every hyperlink needs to contain a tool tip. If you think this requirement is a bit overkill, I should explain that this requirement came from a time when it was really common to use Click Here or Download Here as the text of a hyperlink. Now, you and I both know this is bad practice, and you're probably careful about using meaningful text for your hyperlinks now. But regardless, it is still a requirement. Now, InDesign allows you to meet this requirement. So, I'd like to show you how this works. Now, I'm starting this video with the 0504 document open. And down here in this paragraph, you'll notice that I have a hyperlink. Now, while I'm here, I had brought this up in a previous video where we were talking about multi-line hyperlinks and the challenges that we face with that. One of my suggestions was... And again, I should point out, a lot of people might be asking, "Well, why don't you have the hyperlink "say Ball Mason Jars "and then have that be the link?" Well, there is a legitimate reason for having implicit hyperlinks in a document. And that reason is often because a lot of times, these documents are going to go to print. And for somebody who's reading this on ink and paper, they literally need the full URL to be able to get there.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-alt-text-for-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=94)** If they see a link that says Ball Mason Jars Website, they're going to have to figure it out on their own. So, one of my recommendations was if you do need hyperlinks spelled out, take advantage of something like Bitly to shorten the link. And I'm just going to show you that real quickly. I'm going to grab this hyperlink, and I'm going to copy it. And I'm going to flip over to my web browser where I've gone to the Bitly page. And on Bitly, I can paste my hyperlink in here. And I can hit Enter. And what it's going to do is it's going to create a Bitly link for me. Which is nice and short, easy to kind of type out if I'm trying to get to it while I'm reading it on a piece of paper. So, I'm going to copy this and then replace it in my document. And what that did, as you could see, is it avoided the multi-line hyperlink. And I'll just go back to my web browser here to just show you that if I paste that Bitly link in there, that of course is going to take me to the Ball Mason jars webpage. So, the Bitly link works. Always a good idea to double check it before you put it in there. And then what I'm going to do here, to create the hyperlink in InDesign, we're going to leverage the Hyperlinks panel. So if you go to your Window menu, go down to Interactive, you can choose Hyperlinks.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-alt-text-for-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=188)** And I'll go ahead and dock this over here with my other panels. And the way that you use this hyperlink is you'll select the link that you want to become a hyperlink. And then I'm going to click the plus sign at the bottom of the Hyperlinks panel. And InDesign is pretty smart. It's just going to kind of grab the text that you have selected, it's going to put it into the URL field, and that's pretty much all we need to do. Now, one thing I want to point out, and this is definitely a bug in InDesign. What I've discovered is that I always turn off Shared Hyperlink Destination. I've run into situations where it can become very problematic in a document. And we've talked about this on the "Chax Chat" podcast multiple times. And my recommendation is to just turn it off. Otherwise, you can really get into some problems. In addition, down here, if you choose, you can apply a character style to the hyperlink. By default, InDesign does create a character style called Hyperlink. That is the blue underlined appearance. You can make it look however you want. There's no specific requirement there other than the WCAG requirement that says a hyperlink needs to have a unique appearance so that people can identify that it is in fact a hyperlink. Now, getting back to adding alt text
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-alt-text-for-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=281)** for our hyperlinks. If you click on the Accessibility button within the Hyperlink dialogue box, you're going to get a field here that says alt text. And this allows you to provide a description for where this website is going to go. Now, my approach to this, what I like to do. Way up here in your tab, when you hover over it, this displays the title of the webpage. And in my opinion, this is great information to use as the alt text for the hyperlink. Because the title of the webpage should, and I say should very carefully. Little short trivia for you. Do you know the most commonly named webpage in the world? It's called Untitled Page. That's the most common title for a webpage that you're going to find. So if a website is built properly, each webpage should have an appropriate title. And so I like to grab the title of the webpage. I don't know if I can copy this. Yeah, I can't really copy it here. So, I have a script on my computer that I use to extract the title from every webpage. And that way I can easily paste it in. But this is called Canning & Preserving 101, Ball Mason Jars. So, what I would do is in InDesign I'm going to type
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-alt-text-for-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=374)** Canning and Preserving 101. Ball Mason Jars. And that is going to now be the alt text for that hyperlink. So, I'm going to go ahead and click OK. And what I'll do, let me do a Save As on this. That way, again, you can reference my file if you wish. And then I'm going to export this to the PDF format. So if I come over here to my Tags pane, and I drill down here, we can get down to this paragraph and you'll see here is the link that was created. Now, that's just a byproduct of using the hyperlinks panel in InDesign. But if I right click on this and choose Properties. This is a very interesting topic here because when Adobe first implemented this feature, they were adding it to the alternate text field within the link. But then the Standards people, the people who were big on the standards said, "No, that's not where it belongs. "It should be in the Contents entry." And so to see that, if I click on the Edit Tag button and I open this up. I got to go to K Array, Dictionary, Object. Right here. See if I can open this up a little bit. Yeah, there we go. You can see there is the Contents entry that the Hyperlinks panel in InDesign has created. And that does, in fact, satisfy the requirement of having alt text on your hyperlink.
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-alt-text-for-hyperlinks?u=76281980&t=469)** And you see right here is the text that I added in there. So, again, when creating a hyperlink, it makes sense to spend a few more seconds to provide a description of that hyperlink so that you're meeting PDF/UA compliance.

#### [Working with InDesign-drawn objects](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Objects that are drawn in InDesign are treated differently than other objects, such as placed images and text frames. In this video, I'd like to show you the difference as well as some of the capabilities that can be applied to InDesign drawn objects. Now, I'm sure you're looking at this document and you're thinking, "Boy, you know, this is as simple as it gets." But I did this on purpose because I really just want to kind of focus on how InDesign treats different objects, okay? And what I've done here, I've created three circles but each one of them is a different frame type. This shape here is a text frame. This shape here is a graphic frame and this shape here is an unassigned frame. Now, if you're curious to know, you know, how to create these different frames, a couple of things to know, right? The text tool is going to create a text frame though it's not going to create a circular frame, but it will create a text frame. This shape here will create a graphic frame and this shape here will create an unassigned frame. Now, that being said, whatever shape you draw, I'm going to draw another one here, okay? Let's make this one red, just so we can see the difference, right? And so the way in which you can convert a frame to a different type of frame is by selecting it, coming up to the object menu and going to content.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=95)** And this is where you can convert a shape to a text frame, an unassigned frame, or a graphic frame, okay? So that's how you can get to those different frame types. Okay? Now what I want to show you is if I export this PDF, I just want to kind of illustrate the differences between the three, right? So I'm going to go ahead and save this as a PDF, should export super quick, there we go. And what I want to show you, I mean, if I open up my tags pane, right, you're going to see no tags available because there's just no content, okay? However, if I go to my content pane notice that the text frame outputs as a path, the graphic frame outputs as a path, but the unassigned frame automatically gets artifacted. Okay? Now what's the significance of that? Well, when you're trying to meet PDF/UA compliance, one of the errors you're always going to get is when you have untagged paths like these, okay? And to demonstrate this, what I'll do is I'll grab that file I just exported and run it through the PAC checker. And one of the errors you're going to get under the content category is path object not tagged. And if I click on the first one,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=192)** well, that's my empty text frame. The second one is my empty graphic frame. But notice that the unassigned frame always automatically gets artifacted, okay? Now, you can leverage that knowledge as you design your [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md). If you always make sure that if you're just using a shape for a background or for a sidebar, fill box or whatever it might be, if you take the time and always make sure that you set those frames to be an unassigned frame, they're automatically going to get artifacted, which is going to save you time when you're trying to meet compliance, okay? So, you know, my recommendation is leverage that unassigned frame. I mean, I can't tell you how many documents I look at where people have used a text frame as a background color for an object, and it doesn't contain any text but they used the text frame. I think one of the reasons that happens is because there's a preference in InDesign. If you go to your edit menu and choose preferences and go to type, there is a preference that says, "Type tool converts frames to text frames." Right? And that is enabled by default. I'm not on my machine, but when I'm on my computer I always have this disabled, because when that's turned on
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=286)** if you accidentally click on an object with a type tool, it's going to convert it to a text frame. And I think that's often how that happens in the documents that I'm looking at. So keep that in mind, the different frame types will output in a different way. Now, the other benefit we have when working with InDesign drawn objects, you know, a lot of times we will hand-draw a flow chart in InDesign or we'll hand-draw a bar chart or something like that. One of the features of InDesign is that if you select an object and you go to the object menu and choose object export options, if you go to the alt text category and set the alt text source to custom, you can add alternate text to this shape. So I'm going to type something like, this is my beautiful shape, right? That's my alt text, okay? And by doing that you are instructing InDesign to convert that object to a figure that contains the alt text that you added. So I'm going to go ahead and export this to a PDF and when this opens, if I go to my tags pane, you're going to notice that no tags are available.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=383)** And you're like, "Well, boy, that's interesting." Right? Now, watch what happens if I go back to InDesign and I do the same thing on this object. Okay. Re-export. And now if I go to my tags tree, you're going to see that that object has output as a figure. And if I right-click on that and go to properties, the alt text that I added is in there and it has been tagged as a figure, okay? So what you need to know and to just kind of illustrate this, if I take this object and I go to object, content, unassigned and let me make sure that it retained, yep, it retained my alternate text, if I export this now,
>
> **[7:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=454)** now I have two figures. So there is power in using the unassigned frame, right? When you use the unassigned frame on its own, the advantage is that it automatically will get artifacted in the exported PDF. And if you have an element that you want to be tagged as a figure, make sure it's an unassigned frame and then you can export it and it will also be tagged as a figure. I'll show you one last thing and that is that it also works on grouped objects. So I'm going to do a copy, paste in place.
>
> **[8:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=510)** Let's do something fun here.
>
> **[8:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=525)** If I take all of these shapes, group them together, go to object export options, and add alt text...
>
> **[9:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=546)** When I export this to a PDF you will see that it also works on groups,
>
> **[9:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=562)** provided that it is an unassigned frame. If I were to take this object and let me copy it,
>
> **[9:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=579)** I'm going to ungroup it, object, content, graphic. If I group it again and add alt text to this,
>
> **[10:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/working-with-indesign-drawn-objects?u=76281980&t=609)** it did actually work on a group of graphic frames. So it actually does work on a group but on an individual graphic frame, it does not. So leverage InDesign's natural treatment of InDesign drawn objects to work more efficiently and to force InDesign to output content as you want it to.

### 6. PDF/UA Remediation

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understanding PDF/UA](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/understanding-pdf-ua?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/understanding-pdf-ua?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this video, I wanted to talk about understanding PDF/UA. The UA in PDF/UA stands for Universal Accessibility and PDF/UA is a recognized standard for identifying if a PDF file is, in fact, accessible and compliant. The PDF/UA standard is a world-recognized standard for PDF accessibility compliance. The PDF/UA standard is an ISO Standard. It's part of ISO Standard 14289-1 and it is a recognized standard, again, for validating and confirming that a PDF document is accessible. The PDF/UA standard differs a bit from the WCAG Standard. Where the WCAG Standard is somewhat theoretical in nature and leaves a lot to interpretation, the PDF/UA Standard is a much more technical standard and the PDF/UA standard offers specific benchmarks that determines if our PDF documents are accessible. So you may be asking, "How do we check our documents against the PDF/UA standard?" Well, the PAC 2021 checker is a freely available tool, available for the [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) platform and you can download it right now if you go to pdfua.foundation,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/understanding-pdf-ua?u=76281980&t=94)** you can download the PAC 2021 checker right there. For the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of this chapter, we're going to be utilizing the PAC 2021 checker to check our documents against the standard and to identify problems in our file and throughout this chapter, I'm also going to show you how to fix common errors that you may see when running documents through the PAC 2021 checker.

> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) (1), [Rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) (1)

#### [Fixing element used as a root element error](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-element-used-as-a-root-element-error?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-element-used-as-a-root-element-error?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] This next error we get when our tags are not properly nested inside of a root tag. It's pretty easy to fix. However, the situation generates a lot of what I call false positives. It creates a bunch of errors in the PAC 2021 checker that look like a big deal, but really aren't. Let me show you how to fix this problem. So I've already run the 06_05.pdf file through the PAC 2021 checker. And if I take a look at the results in detail and you go down to the Logical Structure area, you'll notice that in the structure elements we have some errors, but I really want to point out under the category of Structure Tree. You'll notice that the TOC structure element says TOC element used as a root element. It also says H1 used as a root element. It also says H2 and H3 and all of these, right? And so this looks super ugly, guys, right? I mean, look, we have 212 errors under the P tags. And you're like, "Oh my gosh, what's going on?" So if we go to our PDF file, and we open our Tags tree, one of the requirements of PDF UA is that there's a root element in the Tags tree. And if you're not familiar with that term, a root element is an element that contains all other tags. And so what's happening here is all of these tags are living at the root element
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-element-used-as-a-root-element-error?u=76281980&t=96)** of the tag structure. And that's why we're getting all these crazy errors. The PAC checker's not smart enough to just say, "Hey, you're missing a root tag." Instead, it generates all these extra errors that look really scary, but really aren't. So, in a normal document, the root element is a tag called document, okay? And so what we could do, I'm just going to like click up here on this P tag. I'm going to right click and say New Tag. And in the Type dropdown menu, I'm going to choose Document. Okay, so it created that new Document tag, and I'm just going to slide that Document tag all the way up to the top so that it's the first tag in there. And then what I'm going to do, and this might take a couple of seconds, because we're selecting a whole bunch of tags, but I'm going to click on the first tag. Or maybe I'll do it this way. I'm going to click on the last tag, then I'm going to scroll up to the top and Shift + click on the first tag. And Acrobat's going to crank through this and select all these tags. And then with all those tags selected, I'm going to drag them up and nest them inside of that Document tag. And now you can see that all of those tags are nested inside of the Document tag, which is now behaving as the root tag, okay? So what I'll do is I'll do a Save As on this, and I'll call this one 05_06_finish,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-element-used-as-a-root-element-error?u=76281980&t=191)** again, so that if you want to take a look at my file, you can. And then I'm going to drag this onto the PAC checker. And if I look at my results in detail, and now go down to Structure Tree, see how most of these are now passing, right? I'm no longer getting the root element error. I'm getting some different ones, but all of those root errors have now been fixed. So to be fair, most source applications do put all of your tags in a root tag. However, you're likely to encounter this error in documents that you tag from scratch. So watch out for it and fix it if you notice it.

#### [Fixing inappropriate use of an element error](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-inappropriate-use-of-an-element-error?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-inappropriate-use-of-an-element-error?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The inappropriate use of an element error occurs either when an element is in an illegal position in the tags tree or when an element's placement attribute is incorrect. Let's take a look at how to address these problems when they occur. Now, the first thing I'm going to do is run this file through my pack checker, the file I'm working on is 06_06, so I'm going to go ahead and run this through here. And if we look at our results in detail and go to the structure tree, you'll see that I have possible inappropriate use of a P structure element. This one's similar. This one, it's calling invalid use of a TBody structure element, this one is inappropriate use of a note, and then we have inappropriate use of a figure, okay? So let's see how we can fix these errors. So if I click on this error, you can see it's pointing to the footnote on page 8. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to jump to page 8 in my PDF, and I'm going to find that tag, right. Now, again, what we're looking at here, this is a problem with the source application, okay? You would not know this other than me telling it to you, (laughs) but no tags are allowed inside of a note element. The only thing that you can have is marked content. And while I'm talking about that, the same thing applies to the caption tag as well, okay?
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-inappropriate-use-of-an-element-error?u=76281980&t=95)** So the source application created the note, which was correct, but then it put a P tag inside of the note. So to fix this, I'm just going to grab the marked content out of the P tag, still keeping it in the note, and then I could just delete that empty P tag, okay? Then let's look at our other error. We have the P tag, then we have, oh, on page 19, invalid use of TBody, okay. So let's jump over to our PDF and go to page 19, and if I find this, what's happening here in the source application, because I didn't define a row header, there's no THead tag, right? And the problem is it automatically made a TBody but you can't have a TBody unless you have a THead, right? So to be fair, the THead, TBody, and TFooter are all optional tags. They're not at all required. So to fix this, I'm just going to move the rows out of the TBody and then we'll just delete that empty TBody, right? That'll take care of that. The next one is the note itself, okay? And you know what, okay, this is a good time to cover this. The note itself, we're getting in error, and also on the figures, let me see, starting at page 1 to page 13, okay. So this is a little bit of a different situation,
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-inappropriate-use-of-an-element-error?u=76281980&t=191)** and I want to explain this to you. Let's go back to page 8. And so if I find that tag, right? Again, knowledge is everything, guys, right? And so in this case, you know, you're like, "Well, I don't know what's going on. I mean, all it is a note tag. It's not nested inside of anything but I'm still getting this error." And so what's really useful to know, right, is that a tag can have a couple of different placement attributes applied to it, right? Some tags are in line, right, and in line means that it'll be nested inside of another tag. Good example is a span tag, right? A span tag by default is an inline level element. If you put a span tag at the first level under the root element like here, you would get an error because a span tag does not belong at that level, okay? Now, in our case, the note tag, the default placement attribute of a note is to be in line but because we have it at the first level under the root element, it needs to be a block level element, okay? And so the way that we fix that is we're going to right click on the note tag and choose Properties, and if you click Edit Attribute Objects, this is going to bring up your Attributes dialog box, and what we're going to do is we're going to click New Item,
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-inappropriate-use-of-an-element-error?u=76281980&t=286)** and that's going to create this guy here, and then we're going to click New Item again. And the key value is going to be Placement with a capital P, and then the value is going to be Block with a capital B, and we're going to click OK and OK again, and that is going to take care of that problem. Now, you might have also noticed that I was getting a similar error on the figures, right? The same rule applies with figures. When they live at the first level under the root element, they need to be block level elements. So I'll go to edit attribute objects, add the Placement block attribute, there we go, and I have to do that for a couple more here. Here's this one. You want to be careful that you spell it correctly. If you misspell it, it's not going to work. I think that's all I need, right? So what I'm going to do, let's do a save as on this, and I'll call this 06_06_finish, right, and then let's run that through our pack checker. And if you notice now, the structure tree category, I don't even need to dive into it, that entire category is passing,
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-inappropriate-use-of-an-element-error?u=76281980&t=379)** and that's where all of our, just to show you, if I go to results in detail, that's where all of our errors were occurring, right? So by me moving items out of their parent container and/or applying the placement attribute appropriately, I've addressed the inappropriate use error and I've fixed that problem. So keep that in mind when working in your own documents and when you run into this problem, just remember it's either in an illegal position or it just needs a placement attribute added to it.

#### [Role mapping issues](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/role-mapping-issues?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/role-mapping-issues?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Role mapping issues are not nearly as common as other ones, but when they do pop up, a lot of users are at a loss as to what the problem is. In this video, I'd like to introduce you to the role map, explain what it is, and explain why some role mapping can be problematic. So I'm going to go ahead and run this file through my pack checker. We're working with 0607 in this video. And we'll run this through here. And I'm going to go to results in detail. And you can see there is a category called role mapping. And, if we open that up, we're getting an error called circular role mapping. And if I open that up, it's saying that I'll probably have to look at the tool tip. Structure type artifact is mapped in a circular fashion. The pack errors are not always as explanatory as I'd like them to be, but I'm going to explain this one to you. So, in this file in Acrobat, let's go to the tags pane. And if we click on the option button at the top here, I'm going to choose edit role map. And, inside of here, if you open up the document roles, what a role map is, is it's mapping what an object is called in the source application to a standard object in the PDF file. Okay? Now again, this one came out of [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md), but when you look at a document from InDesign,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/role-mapping-issues?u=76281980&t=95)** InDesign is it a lot more logical in my opinion because what you're going to see in the role mapping is you'll see something like main heading, and then to the right it's going to say H1. And so what that means is that the main heading style in InDesign is mapping to an H1 in the pdf, right? That's exactly what it's doing. And so, the role map is literally just kind of a translation that says, hey, the source application calls an object footer in the source application. Here in the pdf we're just going to make it a section tag, or in the source application we have a thing called text box, but in the PDF we're going to map it to a thing called section. And that's because the PDF format does not have an object called text box, or EndNote, or chart. And so we have to map it to the appropriate thing in the PDF file. And so, let's go back to that error again. Yeah. So it's complaining about a role map called artifact. That is the clue that we're looking for. And when it says a circular mapping, what that means is that you have an object that is mapping to itself. And that's why they call it a circular mapping because it's not mapping to something different, it's mapping to itself. And so, up here you'll notice, and this is kind of weird but,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/role-mapping-issues?u=76281980&t=187)** we have an item that was in Word called artifact and it's mapping it to something called artifact in the pdf. And the circular mapping is not allowed. You cannot map something to itself. So honestly, what I'm going to do is I'm going to click on this and I'm going to click on delete item to get rid of that because that's the role map that is the offending item. And I'm going to go ahead and click okay. And let's do a save as on this, and I'll call this one 0607 finish, of course, right? And now, if we go back here and I run 0607 finish through here, you'll notice that the role mapping category is now passing, okay? So, I'll be honest with you guys, you're not going to spend a whole lot of time in the role mapping panel. In my experience, I'm in there few and far between, but every now and then it does come up, right? It does come up and it does require me to go in there to address it. So, the good news is that when you get a role mapping error it's going to tell you exactly which one is the offender. To fix, you just need to open the role mapping panel and make the appropriate adjustment to address that problem.

#### [Fixing alternative descriptions for annotations error](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-alternative-descriptions-for-annotations-error?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-alternative-descriptions-for-annotations-error?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The PDF/UA standard requires that all hyperlinks contain alternative text for that hyperlink. Again, this may seem a bit like overkill but it stems from a time when it was really common to use click here or download here as the text for a hyperlink. Those phrases tell the user nothing about where they're going, hence the requirement for alternative text. The requirement, in my opinion is a bit outdated because most users know and now use meaningful text for their hyperlinks. If you too use meaningful text the good news is that this is going to be a really easy fix. So I'm going to go ahead and run my file through the pack checker. So I have the 0608 file. Go ahead and run this through here. And there's literally a category down here called alternate descriptions, and if you open up the results in detail, you'll notice that I actually have 87 errors, right? And that seems like a lot. But where this is really flagging a lot of them is it's coming from the table of contents. Remember, when you use the table of contents feature in [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) or in InDesign, every item of the TOC becomes an active link that a sighted user can click on or a user of assistive technology can follow when they're reading the content. So we have 87 errors, 87 hyperlinks that need alternate text.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-alternative-descriptions-for-annotations-error?u=76281980&t=93)** Now, the good thing is that your table of contents contains meaningful text, right? I mean, this is perfect, so no need to worry there, right? Your table of contents is already using meaningful text, okay? So how do we fix this problem? Well, we're going to go to our pre-flight panel again. So you can go to the print production tool or as I showed before if I just type "PRE" in the search tools right here is my pre-flight panel that I can open up. And once again, make sure you have PDF standards chosen and that you click on the wrench icon. And then what we're going to do is we're going to scroll down a little bit and down under the interactive elements and properties category, there's actually only one fix up there, and it's the one we need, right? And the fix up is create content entry for link annotations. And again, I know this description, aside from me telling you that this is the one to use, you may not have known that that's what this was for, but in a previous video I talked about how the PDF standard wants you to add a contents entry to the link that provides the alternate text. They actually don't want you to put the alternate text in the alternate text field. I know, confusing right? Anyway, we're going to select this fix up and I'm going to click the fix button. And of course Acrobat is going to want me to save a version of this.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/fixing-alternative-descriptions-for-annotations-error?u=76281980&t=186)** So I will call this the finish version. And again guys, the reason why this works is because we've been cognizant about using meaningful text for our hyperlinks. We're not using click here, we're not using download here. Because if you did and you ran this fix up it would technically pass but it would still be wrong because now you're just putting the alternate text of click here on the link text that says, click here. You're still not providing any useful information to the user. So, key item here guys, use meaningful text for your hyperlinks. Once again, the pre-flight panel comes to the rescue and fixes our problem in mere seconds as long as you use meaningful texts for those hyperlinks.

#### [Adding the PDF/UA entry](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-the-pdf-ua-entry?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-the-pdf-ua-entry?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The PDF/UA identifier is a stamp of approval of sorts that basically indicates that your file meets the PDF/UA Standard. I always do this step last, because I only add it when everything in my file is, in fact, PDF/UA compliant. The PDF/UA identifier is very much a task of the honor system, because you can absolutely add the identifier to a file that is not compliant. That does not, of course, mean that it is compliant. However, it is possible to add it even if the file does not meet conformance. I don't recommend doing that, though. So I'm starting this video with the 06-13 file open and I'm going to go ahead and run this through the PAC Checker just so we can see where we are. So I'll grab this file and you'll notice that we have one remaining item, of course, that does not pass and if we look at the results in detail, it's indicating that the PDF/UA identifier is currently missing. So to fix that, we are going to go back to Adobe Acrobat and we're going to open up our Preflight panel again. So I'm going to go ahead and launch that. And when the Preflight panel opens, if you scroll down under Document info and [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md), you're going to see an item that says, "Set PDF/UA-1 one entry." So that is the one that we want to use to add the PDF/UA identifier.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/adding-the-pdf-ua-entry?u=76281980&t=94)** So I'm going to go ahead and click on the Fix button and I'll go ahead and save this one as the finish version, of course. And you can see one object was created and it was the setting of the PDF/UA entry. So I'm going to go ahead and close this. And if we go back to our PAC Checker, I'm going to grab the finish version and I'm going to run this through the PAC 2021 Checker and you can see now that my file completely is meeting the PDF/UA Standard. And as I had mentioned before, you can click on the PDF Report button and that will create a PDF report confirming that you are complying to the PDF/UA Standard and this is a great thing to supply to your client just to give them peace of mind to know that your file does, in fact, meet the requirements. If I click on the WCAG tab, it is also meeting the WCAG Standard as well. So we have now completed the process of making our document PDF/UA compliant. So again, by leveraging the Preflight panel, we can easily add the PDF/UA-1 entry to a file once it meets PDF/UA compliance.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Why is PDF/UA not verified?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/why-is-pdf-ua-not-verified?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/why-is-pdf-ua-not-verified?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the most common questions that I get asked about meeting PDF/UA compliance is that once you've remediated a document and it is compliant, why does the status still show up as not verified? Well, let's take a closer look at that. So I have the 0614 file open, and what I'd like you to do is over here in the navigation pane, I want you to click on the standards pane to open it up. And if you're not seeing that, go ahead and right-click and then choose standards from the list. Now, the standards pane, if you look at the top you could see that the standard that is applied to this file is PDF/UA-1 and the ISO standard for PDF/UA is ISO 14289-1. But what really gets people is this line here that says, "Status not yet verified." And if you're at all interested in knowing more about this message, we talked about this on the CHAX Chat Podcast. And we had a guest on, his name was Leonard Rosenthal and he kind of explained the why behind this. But fundamentally, there's no way for Acrobat to check the file and verify that it is in fact, PDF/UA compliant. That there's no mechanism available to make that happen. I almost wish they would just get rid of that message
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/why-is-pdf-ua-not-verified?u=76281980&t=95)** because it creates more confusion than it provides answers. The useful information in this pane is that it is in fact, using the PDF/UA standard, and it is applied to this document, but the status is not yet verified. It's really just saying that there's no machine learning, there's no mechanism by which it can validate your file against the PDF/UA standard. So when you see this message, don't fret, it's really neither good or bad. The not verified message doesn't really mean a whole lot. It certainly doesn't mean that your document does not meet PDF/UA compliance but it doesn't mean that it does either. So rely on the PAC 2021 Checker to verify compliance, not the standards pane.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

#### [PDF/UA resources](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/pdf-ua-resources?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/pdf-ua-resources?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So I wanted to leave you with some resources that you could go to if you wanted to learn more about PDF/UA. We covered a lot of material in this course. However, there's always more to learn, right. And so one resource I'd like to direct you to is the Matterhorn Protocol and you could find this at [pdfa.org](https://pdfa.org). And the Matterhorn Protocol was designed to promote the adoption of PDF/UA, okay. And on this page, you can click the download button which will actually download a document that kind of guides you through the Conformance Testing Model. The Matterhorn Protocol, it shows you like what are some failure conditions and clarifies how a document is supposed to be tagged. Another good resource is the PD/UA reference suite. This is a pretty good resource. What I like about this is if you click the download button it'll give you a folder of files of properly tagged files, okay. And so you can open up any of these PDF files and inspect the tag structure to kind of look at how this document was tagged and evaluate it and just kind learn a little bit more, and get some good examples of how a good document is tagged. And then another really great resource is something called the Tagged PDF Best Practice Guide. And it focuses on the syntax of the PDF/UA standard.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/pdf-ua-resources?u=76281980&t=97)** And again, if you click the download button it will give you, it's a 70 page document covering everything about PDF/UA, talking about layout attributes, table attributes, grouping elements, all different elements of a PDF file. So really good resources and just good resources to have to learn more and just kind of grow your knowledge about PDF/UA in general. I hope you enjoy that, guys, and we'll see you soon.

#### [Next steps for accessibility](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/next-steps-for-accessibility?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/next-steps-for-accessibility?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Hey guys, you made it. I'm really proud of you guys for sticking with me through this course. I hope you learned a lot. I hope you learned some good tips and solutions to problems you've been having. If you're looking for next steps, there's some things I'd like to share with you. One thing I'd like to show you is our website, which is [accessibilityunraveled.com](https://accessibilityunraveled.com). We have weekly webinars on various accessibility topics. We also provide training and consulting. If you or your organization is looking to implement accessibility and you need help, you need people trained, you need guidance, please reach out to us. If you're also interested in Access [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md), Access PDF or MadetoTag, please reach out to us. We could provide you with licenses on that. Another great resource is the CHAX Chat Podcast. We try to make this podcast weekly. Holidays sometimes throw us off a little bit in our schedule, but we are up into, I think we're almost at like 80 episodes. We've been doing this for almost two years. So check it out. It's totally free, and you can get it on any of the major podcast platforms, you know, Spotify, Apple Music, you name it. Another great resource that a lot of people are leveraging is the PDF Accessibility [Facebook](../../Glossary/Service/Facebook.md) group. Now, again, I know some of you're saying, well, I'm really a Facebook user, and that's totally okay. But I'll tell you, it's almost worth joining Facebook
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/next-steps-for-accessibility?u=76281980&t=96)** just for access to this group. We have over 3000 members at this point, and it's just a fantastic resource to go and ask your questions, ask for guidance. Like I said, there's 3000 people out there who can respond to you and give you answers. I also recommend, I mean, if you're looking to kind of keep expanding your knowledge base and expanding what you know, there's a lot of great conferences that you can attend. One of my favorites is the Creative Pro Conference. This year, it's June 5th to 9th in Phoenix, Arizona. It's a great resource. There are accessibility sessions. There are also creative sessions, so check it out. It's really a great event. It's fairly small size, so it's nice and intimate and really a great event. Similar to Creative Pro, every year we have The Design Accessibility Summit, and this is a accessibility-focused conference. It is an online-only event, which may make it easier for some of you to attend. I speak there, [DAX](../../Skills/Data%20Science/DAX.md) Castro speaks there, a lot of great speakers. We also speak at Creative Pro, and I highly encourage you to check it out, covers topics on PDF Accessibility, InDesign, Acrobat, [PowerPoint](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20PowerPoint.md), and Word, really, really great information. If you're looking for like a heavy duty accessibility conference, CSUN is a great conference to go to.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-accessible-pdfs-21974253/next-steps-for-accessibility?u=76281980&t=192)** This year, it is March 13th to 17th in Anaheim, California. I'm telling you, if you really want to grow your knowledge and learn more about accessibility overall and about the experience of people with disabilities, it's a fantastic conference to go to. And then another great accessibility conference is called Accessing Higher Ground. This conference is typically in Denver, and next year's date is already up here. It's November 6th to 10th, 2023, another great conference on accessibility. Again, Dax Castro and myself speak at this event. We also speak at CSUN, and it's just a great event to go to to learn more about accessibility in general. So I hope you guys enjoy these resources, check them out. And, as I said before, I'd love to hear what you think of the course and always am looking for feedback and want to know what you think. So I hope to hear from you guys soon.
